One problem with the plastic daisy wheel was that they were not always durable. To solve this problem, more durable metal daisy wheels were made available (but at a slightly higher price).
      </p>
      <p>
       These and similar electronic typewriters were in essence dedicated
       <a href="/wiki/Word_processor" title="Word processor">
        word processors
       </a>
       with either single-line
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LCD" title="LCD">
        LCD
       </a>
       displays or multi-line
       <a href="/wiki/Cathode_ray_tube" title="Cathode ray tube">
        CRT
       </a>
       displays, built-in
       <a href="/wiki/Line_editor" title="Line editor">
        line editors
       </a>
       in ROM, a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spelling_checker" title="Spelling checker">
        spelling
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Grammar_checker" title="Grammar checker">
        grammar checker
       </a>
       , a few kilobytes of internal
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RAM" title="RAM">
        RAM
       </a>
       and optional cartridge, magnetic card or diskette external memory-storage devices for storing text and even document formats. Text could be entered a line or paragraph at a time and edited using the display and built-in software tools before being committed to paper.
      </p>
      <p>
       Unlike the Selectrics and earlier models, these really were "electronic" and relied on integrated circuits and multiple electromechanical components. These typewriters were sometimes called
       <i>
        display typewriters
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        dedicated word processors
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        word-processing typewriters
       </i>
       , though the latter term was also frequently applied to less sophisticated machines that featured only a tiny, sometimes just single-row display. Sophisticated models were also called
       <i>
        word processors
       </i>
       , though today that term almost always denotes a type of software program. Manufacturers of such machines included
       <a href="/wiki/Brother_Industries" title="Brother Industries">
        Brother
       </a>
       (Brother WP1 and WP500 etc., where WP stood for word processor),
       <a href="/wiki/Canon_Inc." title="Canon Inc.">
        Canon
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Canon_Cat" title="Canon Cat">
        Canon Cat
       </a>
       ),
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smith-Corona" title="Smith-Corona">
        Smith-Corona
       </a>
       (PWP, i.e. Personal Word Processor line)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Philips" title="Philips">
        Philips
       </a>
       /
       <a href="/wiki/Magnavox" title="Magnavox">
        Magnavox
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/VideoWriter" title="VideoWriter">
        VideoWriter
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
        <div style="width: 155px">
         <div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
          <div style="margin:37.5px auto;">
           <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Type.jpg">
            <img alt="" data-file-height="1132" data-file-width="1800" height="75" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Type.jpg/120px-Type.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Type.jpg/180px-Type.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Type.jpg/240px-Type.jpg 2x" width="120"/>
           </a>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallerytext">
          <p>
           Electronic typewriter – the final stage in typewriter development. A 1989
           <a href="/wiki/Canon_Inc." title="Canon Inc.">
            Canon
           </a>
           Typestar 110
          </p>
         </div>
        </div>
       </li>
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
        <div style="width: 155px">
         <div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
          <div style="margin:35px auto;">
           <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brother_WP1-IMG_6991.jpg">
            <img alt="" data-file-height="3744" data-file-width="5616" height="80" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Brother_WP1-IMG_6991.jpg/120px-Brother_WP1-IMG_6991.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Brother_WP1-IMG_6991.jpg/180px-Brother_WP1-IMG_6991.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Brother_WP1-IMG_6991.jpg/240px-Brother_WP1-IMG_6991.jpg 2x" width="120"/>
           </a>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallerytext">
          <p>
           The Brother WP1, an electronic typewriter complete with a small screen and a
           <a href="/wiki/Floppy_disk" title="Floppy disk">
            floppy disk
           </a>
           reader
          </p>
         </div>
        </div>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_an_era">
        End of an era
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: End of an era">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The 1970s and early 1980s were a time of transition for typewriters and word processors. At one point in time, most small-business offices would be completely 'old-style', while large corporations and government departments would already be 'new-style'; other offices would have a mixture. The pace of change was so rapid that it was common for clerical staff to have to learn several new systems, one after the other, in just a few years. While such rapid change is commonplace today, and is taken for granted, this was not always so; in fact, typewriting technology changed very little in its first 80 or 90 years.
      </p>
      <p>
       Due to falling sales, IBM sold its typewriter division in 1991 to
       <a href="/wiki/Lexmark" title="Lexmark">
        Lexmark
       </a>
       , completely exiting from a market it once dominated.
      </p>
      <p>
       The increasing dominance of
       <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">
        personal computers
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Desktop_publishing" title="Desktop publishing">
        desktop publishing
       </a>
       , the introduction of low-cost, truly high-quality
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laser_printer" title="Laser printer">
        laser
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inkjet_printer" title="Inkjet printer">
        inkjet printer
       </a>
       technologies, and the pervasive use of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Web_publishing" title="Web publishing">
        web publishing
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E-mail" title="E-mail">
        e-mail
       </a>
       and other electronic communication techniques have largely replaced typewriters in the United States. Still, as of 2009
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , typewriters continued to be used by a number of government agencies and other institutions in the US, where they are primarily used to fill preprinted forms. According to a Boston typewriter repairman quoted by
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Boston_Globe" title="The Boston Globe">
         The Boston Globe
        </a>
       </i>
       , "Every maternity ward has a typewriter, as well as funeral homes".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A fairly major typewriter user is the City of New York, which in 2008 purchased several thousand typewriters, mostly for use by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Police_Department" title="New York Police Department">
        New York Police Department
       </a>
       , at the total cost of $982,269. Another $99,570 was spent in 2009 for the maintenance of the existing typewriters. New York police officers would use the machines to type property and evidence vouchers on
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_paper" title="Carbon paper">
        carbon paper
       </a>
       forms.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A rather specialized market for typewriters exists due to the regulations of many correctional systems in the US, where prisoners are prohibited from having computers or telecommunication equipment, but are allowed to own typewriters. The Swintec corporation (headquartered in
       <a href="/wiki/Moonachie,_New_Jersey" title="Moonachie, New Jersey">
        Moonachie, New Jersey
       </a>
       ), which, as of 2011, still produced typewriters at its overseas factories (in
       <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">
        Japan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">
        Indonesia
       </a>
       , and/or
       <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">
        Malaysia
       </a>
       ), manufactures a variety of typewriters for use in prisons, made of clear plastic (to make it harder for prisoners to hide prohibited items inside it). As of 2011, the company had contracts with prisons in 43 US states.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In April 2011, Godrej and Boyce, a
       <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">
        Mumbai
       </a>
       -based manufacturer of mechanical typewriters, closed its doors, leading to a flurry of erroneous news reports that the "world's last typewriter factory" had shut down.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The reports were quickly debunked.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rohrlich_55-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rohrlich-55">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In November 2012, Brother's UK factory manufactured what it claimed to be the last typewriter ever made in the UK; the typewriter was donated to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/London_Science_Museum" title="London Science Museum">
        London Science Museum
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Russian typewriters use
       <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_script" title="Cyrillic script">
        Cyrillic
       </a>
       , which has made the ongoing
       <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_language" title="Azerbaijani language">
        Azerbaijani
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_alphabet" title="Azerbaijani alphabet">
        reconversion
       </a>
       from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyrillic_alphabet" title="Cyrillic alphabet">
        Cyrillic
       </a>
       to
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">
        Latin alphabet
       </a>
       more difficult. In 1997, the government of
       <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">
        Turkey
       </a>
       offered to donate western typewriters to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_Azerbaijan" title="Republic of Azerbaijan">
        Republic of Azerbaijan
       </a>
       in exchange for more zealous and exclusive promotion of the Latin alphabet for the Azerbaijani language; this offer, however, was declined.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2011)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In Latin America and Africa, mechanical typewriters are still common because they can be used without electrical power. In Latin America, the typewriters used are most often Brazilian models – Brazil continues to produce mechanical (Facit) and electronic (Olivetti) typewriters to the present day.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The 21st century has seen a revival of interest in typewriters among certain subcultures, including makers, steampunks, hipsters, and street poets.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Correction_technologies">
        Correction technologies
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Correction technologies">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TypewriterMiningMuseumPachuca.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1155" data-file-width="1541" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/TypewriterMiningMuseumPachuca.JPG/220px-TypewriterMiningMuseumPachuca.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/TypewriterMiningMuseumPachuca.JPG/330px-TypewriterMiningMuseumPachuca.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/TypewriterMiningMuseumPachuca.JPG/440px-TypewriterMiningMuseumPachuca.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TypewriterMiningMuseumPachuca.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         An Elliott-Fisher book typewriter on display at the
         <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Historic_Archive_and_Museum_of_Mining&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Historic Archive and Museum of Mining (page does not exist)">
          Historic Archive and Museum of Mining
         </a>
         in
         <a href="/wiki/Pachuca" title="Pachuca">
          Pachuca
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
          Mexico
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       According to the standards taught in secretarial schools in the mid-20th century, a
       <a href="/wiki/Business_letter" title="Business letter">
        business letter
       </a>
       was supposed to have no mistakes and no visible corrections.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Accuracy was prized as much as speed. Indeed, typing speeds, as scored in proficiency tests and
       <a href="/wiki/Speed_typing_contest" title="Speed typing contest">
        typewriting speed competitions
       </a>
       , included a deduction of ten words for every mistake. Corrections were, of course, necessary, and many methods were developed.
      </p>
      <p>
       In practice, several methods would often be combined. For example, if six extra copies of a letter were needed, the fluid-corrected original would be photocopied, but only for the two recipients getting "c.c."s; the other four copies, the less-important file copies that stayed in various departments at the office, would be cheaper, hand-erased, less-distinct bond paper copies or even "flimsies" of different colors (tissue papers interleaved with black carbon paper) that were all typed as a "carbon pack" at the same time as the original.
      </p>
      <p>
       In informal applications such as personal letters where low priority was placed on the appearance of the document, or conversely in highly formal applications in which it was important that any corrections be obvious, the backspace key could be used to back up over the error and then overstrike it with hyphens, slashes, Xs, or the like.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Typewriter_erasers">
        Typewriter erasers
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Typewriter erasers">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Triumph_-_Typewriter_Eraser_1960.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1446" data-file-width="1428" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Triumph_-_Typewriter_Eraser_1960.jpg/170px-Triumph_-_Typewriter_Eraser_1960.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Triumph_-_Typewriter_Eraser_1960.jpg/255px-Triumph_-_Typewriter_Eraser_1960.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Triumph_-_Typewriter_Eraser_1960.jpg/340px-Triumph_-_Typewriter_Eraser_1960.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Triumph_-_Typewriter_Eraser_1960.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Triumph typewriter eraser (1960)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The traditional erasing method involved the use of a special
       <i>
        typewriter
        <a href="/wiki/Eraser" title="Eraser">
         eraser
        </a>
       </i>
       made of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hard_rubber" title="Hard rubber">
        hard rubber
       </a>
       that contained an
       <a href="/wiki/Abrasive" title="Abrasive">
        abrasive
       </a>
       material. Some were thin, flat disks, pink or gray, approximately 2 inches (51 mm) in diameter by ⅛ inch (3.2 mm) thick, with a brush attached from the center, while others looked like pink pencils, with a sharpenable eraser at the "lead" end and a stiff
       <a href="/wiki/Nylon" title="Nylon">
        nylon
       </a>
       brush at the other end. Either way, these tools made possible erasure of individual typed letters. Business letters were typed on heavyweight, high-rag-content bond paper, not merely to provide a luxurious appearance, but also to stand up to erasure.
      </p>
      <p>
       Typewriter eraser brushes were necessary for clearing eraser crumbs and paper dust, and using the brush properly was an important element of typewriting skill; if erasure detritus fell into the typewriter, a small buildup could cause the typebars to jam in their narrow supporting grooves.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Eraser_shield">
        Eraser shield
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Eraser shield">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Erasing a set of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_copies" title="Carbon copies">
        carbon copies
       </a>
       was particularly difficult, and called for the use of a device called an
       <i>
        eraser shield
       </i>
       (a thin stainless-steel rectangle about 2 by 3 inches (51 by 76 mm) with several tiny holes in it) to prevent the pressure of erasing on the upper copies from producing carbon smudges on the lower copies. To correct copies, typists had to go from carbon copy to carbon copy, trying not to get their fingers dirty as they leafed through the carbon papers, and moving and repositioning the eraser shield and eraser for each copy.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Erasable_bond">
        Erasable bond
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Erasable bond">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Paper companies produced a special form of typewriter paper called
       <i>
        erasable bond
       </i>
       (for example,
       <a href="/wiki/Eaton%27s_Corrasable_Bond" title="Eaton's Corrasable Bond">
        Eaton's Corrasable Bond
       </a>
       ). This incorporated a thin layer of material that prevented ink from penetrating and was relatively soft and easy to remove from the page. An ordinary soft pencil eraser could quickly produce perfect erasures on this kind of paper. However, the same characteristics that made the paper erasable made the characters subject to smudging due to ordinary friction and deliberate alteration after the fact, making it unacceptable for business correspondence, contracts, or any archival use.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Correction_fluid">
        Correction fluid
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Correction fluid">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Correction_fluid" title="Correction fluid">
        Correction fluid
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In the 1950s and 1960s,
       <a href="/wiki/Correction_fluid" title="Correction fluid">
        correction fluid
       </a>
       made its appearance, under brand names such as
       <a href="/wiki/Liquid_Paper" title="Liquid Paper">
        Liquid Paper
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Wite-Out" title="Wite-Out">
        Wite-Out
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Tipp-Ex" title="Tipp-Ex">
        Tipp-Ex
       </a>
       ; it was invented by
       <a href="/wiki/Bette_Nesmith_Graham" title="Bette Nesmith Graham">
        Bette Nesmith Graham
       </a>
       . Correction fluid was a kind of opaque, white, fast-drying paint that produced a fresh white surface onto which, when dry, a correction could be retyped. However, when held to the light, the covered-up characters were visible, as was the patch of dry correction fluid (which was never perfectly flat, and frequently not a perfect match for the color, texture, and luster of the surrounding paper). The standard trick for solving this problem was
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photocopying" title="Photocopying">
        photocopying
       </a>
       the corrected page, but this was possible only with high quality photocopiers.
      </p>
      <p>
       A different fluid was available for correcting stencils. It sealed up the stencil ready for retyping but did not attempt to color match.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MimeoCorrect_59-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MimeoCorrect-59">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Dry_correction">
        Dry correction
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Dry correction">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Dry correction products (such as
       <a href="/wiki/Correction_paper" title="Correction paper">
        correction paper
       </a>
       ) under brand names such as "Ko-Rec-Type" were introduced in the 1970s and functioned like white carbon paper. A strip of the product was placed over the letters needing correction, and the incorrect letters were retyped, causing the black character to be overstruck with a white overcoat. Similar material was soon incorporated in carbon-film electric typewriter ribbons; like the traditional two-color black-and-red inked ribbon common on manual typewriters, a black and white correcting ribbon became commonplace on electric typewriters. But the black or white coating could be partly rubbed off with handling, so such corrections were generally not acceptable in legal documents.
      </p>
      <p>
       The pinnacle of this kind of technology was the
       <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">
        IBM
       </a>
       Electronic Typewriter series. These machines, and similar products from other manufacturers, used a separate correction ribbon and a character memory. With a single keystroke, the typewriter was capable of automatically backspacing and then overstriking the previous characters with minimal marring of the paper. White cover-up ribbons were used with fabric ink ribbons, or an alternate premium design featured plastic lift-off correction ribbons which were used with carbon film typing ribbons. This latter technology actually lifted the carbon film forming a typed letter, leaving nothing more than a flattened depression in the surface of the paper, with the advantage that no color matching of the paper was needed.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">
        Legacy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Legacy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Keyboard_layouts">
        Keyboard layouts
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Keyboard layouts">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UnderwoodKeyboard.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="283" data-file-width="325" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/UnderwoodKeyboard.jpg/220px-UnderwoodKeyboard.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c7/UnderwoodKeyboard.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:UnderwoodKeyboard.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The "
         <a href="/wiki/QWERTY" title="QWERTY">
          QWERTY
         </a>
         " layout of typewriter keys became a
         <i>
          de facto
         </i>
         standard and continues to be used long after the reasons for its adoption (including reduction of key/lever entanglements) have ceased to apply.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="QWERTY">
        QWERTY
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: QWERTY">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The 1874 Sholes &amp; Glidden typewriters established the "
       <a href="/wiki/QWERTY" title="QWERTY">
        QWERTY
       </a>
       " layout for the letter keys. During the period in which Sholes and his colleagues were experimenting with this invention, other keyboard arrangements were apparently tried, but these are poorly documented.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The QWERTY layout of keys has become the
       <a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">
        de facto
       </a>
       standard for English-language typewriter and computer keyboards. Other languages written in the
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">
        Latin alphabet
       </a>
       sometimes use variants of the QWERTY layouts, such as the French
       <a href="/wiki/AZERTY" title="AZERTY">
        AZERTY
       </a>
       , the Italian
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/QZERTY" title="QZERTY">
        QZERTY
       </a>
       and the German
       <a href="/wiki/QWERTZ" title="QWERTZ">
        QWERTZ
       </a>
       layouts.
      </p>
      <p>
       The QWERTY layout is not the most efficient layout possible for the English language, since it requires a touch-typist to move his or her fingers between rows to type the most common letters. Although the QWERTY keyboard was the most commonly used layout in typewriters, a better, less strenuous keyboard was being searched for throughout the late 1900s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       One popular but unverified
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       explanation for the QWERTY arrangement is that it was designed to reduce the likelihood of internal clashing of typebars by placing commonly used combinations of letters farther from each other inside the machine.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David,_P.A._1986_63-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-David,_P.A._1986-63">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Another story is that the QWERTY layout allowed early typewriter salesmen to impress their customers by being able to easily type out the example word "typewriter" without having learned the full keyboard layout
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="Reliable source needed for the whole sentence (February 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       , because "typewriter" can be spelled purely on the top row of the keyboard. However, there is no evidence to support these claims.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_layouts">
        Other layouts
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Other layouts">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       A number of radically different layouts such as
       <a href="/wiki/Dvorak_Simplified_Keyboard" title="Dvorak Simplified Keyboard">
        Dvorak
       </a>
       have been proposed to reduce the perceived inefficiencies of QWERTY, but none have been able to displace the QWERTY layout; their proponents claim considerable advantages, but so far none has been widely used. The
       <a href="/wiki/Blickensderfer_typewriter" title="Blickensderfer typewriter">
        Blickensderfer typewriter
       </a>
       with its
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DHIATENSOR" title="DHIATENSOR">
        DHIATENSOR
       </a>
       layout may have possibly been the first attempt at optimizing the keyboard layout for efficiency advantages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Many non-Latin alphabets have keyboard layouts that have nothing to do with QWERTY. The Russian layout, for instance, puts the common trigrams ыва, про, and ить on adjacent keys so that they can be typed by rolling the fingers. The Greek layout, on the other hand, is a variant of QWERTY.
      </p>
      <p>
       Typewriters were also made for
       <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_languages" title="East Asian languages">
        East Asian languages
       </a>
       with thousands of characters, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_typewriter" title="Chinese typewriter">
        Chinese
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Japanese_typewriter" title="Japanese typewriter">
        Japanese
       </a>
       . They were not easy to operate, but professional typists used them for a long time until the development of electronic word processors and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laser_printer" title="Laser printer">
        laser printers
       </a>
       in the 1980s.
      </p>
      <p>
       On modern keyboards, the exclamation point is the shifted character on the 1 key, a direct result of the historical fact that these were the last characters to become "standard" on keyboards. Holding the spacebar pressed down usually suspended the carriage advance mechanism (a so-called "dead key" feature), allowing one to superimpose multiple keystrikes on a single location. The ¢ symbol (meaning cents) was located above the number 6 on electric typewriters, while ASCII computer keyboards have ^ instead.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Typewriter_conventions">
        Typewriter conventions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Typewriter conventions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Typerwriter_conventions_text_example.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="525" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Typerwriter_conventions_text_example.gif/220px-Typerwriter_conventions_text_example.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Typerwriter_conventions_text_example.gif/330px-Typerwriter_conventions_text_example.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Typerwriter_conventions_text_example.gif/440px-Typerwriter_conventions_text_example.gif 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Typerwriter_conventions_text_example.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         This typed page uses a number of typographic conventions stemming from the mechanical limitations of the typewriter: two hyphens in place of an
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Em_dash" title="Em dash">
          em dash
         </a>
         , double
         <a href="/wiki/Sentence_spacing" title="Sentence spacing">
          sentence spacing
         </a>
         , straight
         <a href="/wiki/Quotation_mark" title="Quotation mark">
          quotation marks
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Tab_key" title="Tab key">
          tab
         </a>
         indents for paragraphs, and double
         <a href="/wiki/Carriage_return" title="Carriage return">
          carriage returns
         </a>
         between paragraphs
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A number of typographical conventions originate from the widespread use of the typewriter, based on the characteristics and limitations of the typewriter itself. For example, the QWERTY keyboard typewriter did not include keys for the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/En_dash" title="En dash">
        en dash
       </a>
       and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Em_dash" title="Em dash">
        em dash
       </a>
       . To overcome this limitation, users typically typed more than one adjacent hyphen to approximate these symbols. This typewriter convention is still sometimes used today, even though modern computer word processing applications can input the correct en and em dashes for each font type.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Other examples of typewriter practices that are sometimes still used in desktop publishing systems include inserting a
       <a href="/wiki/Sentence_spacing" title="Sentence spacing">
        double space
       </a>
       at the end of a sentence,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the use of straight quotes (or "dumb quotes") as quotation marks and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prime_mark" title="Prime mark">
        prime marks
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The practice of underlining text in place of italics and the use of all capitals to provide emphasis are additional examples of typographical conventions that derived from the limitations of the typewriter keyboard that still carry on today.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Many older typewriters did not include a separate key for the numeral 1 or the exclamation point, and some even older ones also lacked the numeral zero. Typists who trained on these machines learned the habit of using the lowercase letter l ("ell") for the digit 1, and the uppercase O for the zero. A cents symbol (¢) was created by combining (
       <a href="/wiki/Overstrike" title="Overstrike">
        over-striking
       </a>
       ) a lower case 'c' with a slash character (typing 'c', then backspace, then '/').
      </p>
      <p>
       Similarly, the exclamation point was created by combining an apostrophe and a period.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These characters were omitted to simplify design and reduce manufacturing and maintenance costs; they were chosen specifically because they were "redundant" and could be recreated using other keys.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">
        Terminology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Terminology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Some terminology from the typewriter age has survived into the personal computer era. Examples include:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Backspace" title="Backspace">
         backspace
        </a>
        (BS) – a keystroke that moved the cursor backwards one position (on a physical platen, this is the exact opposite of the space key), for the purpose of overtyping a character. This could be for combining characters (e.g. an apostrophe, backspace, and period make an exclamation point—a character missing on some early typewriters), or for correction such as with the correcting tape that developed later.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Carriage_return" title="Carriage return">
         carriage return
        </a>
        (CR) – return to the first column of text and, in some systems, switch to the next line.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cursor_(computers)" title="Cursor (computers)">
         cursor
        </a>
        – a marker used to indicate where the next character will be printed. The cursor, however, was originally a term to describe the clear slider on a
        <a href="/wiki/Slide_rule" title="Slide rule">
         slide rule
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Cut,_copy,_and_paste" title="Cut, copy, and paste">
         cut and paste
        </a>
        – taking text, a numerical table, or an image and pasting it into a document. The term originated when such compound documents were created using manual
        <a href="/wiki/Paste_up" title="Paste up">
         paste up
        </a>
        techniques for typographic
        <a href="/wiki/Page_layout" title="Page layout">
         page layout
        </a>
        . Actual brushes and paste were later replaced by hot-wax machines equipped with cylinders that applied melted adhesive wax to developed prints of "typeset" copy. This copy was then cut out with knives and rulers, and slid into position on layout sheets on slanting layout tables. After the "copy" had been correctly positioned and squared up using a T-square and set square, it was pressed down with a brayer, or roller. The whole point of the exercise was to create so-called "camera-ready copy" which existed only to be photographed and then printed, usually by
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Offset_lithography" title="Offset lithography">
         offset lithography
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Dead_key" title="Dead key">
         dead key
        </a>
        – describes a key that when typed, does not advance the typing position, thus allowing another character to be overstruck on top of the original character. This was typically used to combine
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diacritical_mark" title="Diacritical mark">
         diacritical marks
        </a>
        with letters they modified (e.g.
        <i>
         è
        </i>
        can be generated by first pressing
        <code>
         `
        </code>
        and then
        <code>
         e
        </code>
        ). The dead key feature was often implemented mechanically by having the typist press and hold the space bar while typing the characters to be superimposed.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Line_feed" title="Line feed">
         line feed
        </a>
        (LF), also called "newline" – moving the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cursor_(computers)" title="Cursor (computers)">
         cursor
        </a>
        to the next on-screen line of text in a word processor document.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Shift_key" title="Shift key">
         shift
        </a>
        – a
        <a href="/wiki/Modifier_key" title="Modifier key">
         modifier key
        </a>
        used to type capital letters and other alternate "upper case" characters; when pressed and held down, would shift a typewriter's mechanism to allow a different typebar impression (such as 'D' instead of 'd') to press into the ribbon and print on a page. The concept of a shift key or modifier key was later extended to Ctrl, Alt, and Super ("Windows" or "Apple") keys on modern computer keyboards. The generalized concept of a shift key reached its apotheosis in the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIT" title="MIT">
         MIT
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Space-cadet_keyboard" title="Space-cadet keyboard">
         space-cadet keyboard
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tabulator_key" title="Tabulator key">
         tab
        </a>
        (HT), shortened from "horizontal tab" or "tabulator stop" – caused the print position to advance horizontally to the next pre-set "tab stop." This was used for typing lists and tables with vertical columns of numbers or words. The related term "vertical tab" (VT) never came into widespread use.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tty_(Unix)" title="Tty (Unix)">
         tty
        </a>
        , short for
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teletypewriter" title="Teletypewriter">
         teletypewriter
        </a>
        – used in
        <a href="/wiki/Unix-like" title="Unix-like">
         Unix-like
        </a>
        operating systems to designate a given "terminal".
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       In the above listing, the two-letter codes in parentheses are abbreviations for the
       <a href="/wiki/ASCII" title="ASCII">
        ASCII
       </a>
       characters derived from typewriter usage.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_social_effects">
        Early social effects
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Early social effects">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CantYouSeeImBusyCardCropped.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="885" data-file-width="955" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/CantYouSeeImBusyCardCropped.jpg/170px-CantYouSeeImBusyCardCropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/CantYouSeeImBusyCardCropped.jpg/255px-CantYouSeeImBusyCardCropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/CantYouSeeImBusyCardCropped.jpg/340px-CantYouSeeImBusyCardCropped.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CantYouSeeImBusyCardCropped.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Humorous "Get out! Can't you see I'm busy" postcard (1900s)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       When Remington started marketing typewriters, the company assumed the machine would not be used for composing but for transcribing dictation, and that the person typing would be a woman. The 1800s
       <a href="/wiki/Sholes_and_Glidden_typewriter" title="Sholes and Glidden typewriter">
        Sholes and Glidden typewriter
       </a>
       had floral ornamentation on the case.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During World War I and II, increasing numbers of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Women%27s_roles_in_the_World_Wars" title="Women's roles in the World Wars">
        women
       </a>
       were entering the workforce. In the United States, women often started in the professional workplace as typists. Questions about morals made a salacious businessman making sexual advances to a female typist into a cliché of office life, appearing in vaudeville and movies. Being a typist was considered the right choice for a "good girl" meaning women who present themselves as being chaste and having good conduct.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to the 1900 census, 94.9 percent of stenographers and typists were unmarried women.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The "
       <a href="/wiki/Tijuana_bible" title="Tijuana bible">
        Tijuana bibles
       </a>
       " — adult comic books produced in Mexico for the American market, starting in the 1930s — often featured women typists. In one panel, a businessman in a three-piece suit, ogling his secretary’s thigh, says, "Miss Higby, are you ready for—ahem!—er—dictation?"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ja_34-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ja-34">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Authors_and_writers_who_had_notable_relationships_with_typewriters">
        Authors and writers who had notable relationships with typewriters
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Authors and writers who had notable relationships with typewriters">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_adopters">
        Early adopters
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Early adopters">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Henry_James" title="Henry James">
         Henry James
        </a>
        dictated to a typist.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ja_34-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-ja-34">
          [33]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain">
         Mark Twain
        </a>
        claimed in
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mark_Twain%27s_Autobiography" title="Mark Twain's Autobiography">
         his autobiography
        </a>
        that he was the first important writer to present a publisher with a typewritten manuscript, for
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Tom_Sawyer" title="The Adventures of Tom Sawyer">
          The Adventures of Tom Sawyer
         </a>
        </i>
        (1876). Research showed that Twain's memory was incorrect and that the first book submitted in typed form was
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Life_on_the_Mississippi" title="Life on the Mississippi">
          Life on the Mississippi
         </a>
        </i>
        (1883, also by Twain).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
         <a href="#cite_note-75">
          [74]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Others">
        Others
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Others">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TheFaulknerPortable.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="532" data-file-width="800" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/TheFaulknerPortable.jpg/220px-TheFaulknerPortable.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/TheFaulknerPortable.jpg/330px-TheFaulknerPortable.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/TheFaulknerPortable.jpg/440px-TheFaulknerPortable.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TheFaulknerPortable.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/William_Faulkner" title="William Faulkner">
          William Faulkner
         </a>
         's
         <a href="/wiki/Underwood_Typewriter_Company" title="Underwood Typewriter Company">
          Underwood
         </a>
         Universal Portable sits in his office at
         <a href="/wiki/Rowan_Oak" title="Rowan Oak">
          Rowan Oak
         </a>
         , which is now maintained by the
         <a href="/wiki/University_of_Mississippi" title="University of Mississippi">
          University of Mississippi
         </a>
         in
         <a href="/wiki/Oxford,_Mississippi" title="Oxford, Mississippi">
          Oxford
         </a>
         as a museum.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/William_S._Burroughs" title="William S. Burroughs">
         William S. Burroughs
        </a>
        wrote in some of his novels—and possibly believed—that "a machine he called the 'Soft Typewriter' was writing our lives, and our books, into existence," according to a book review in
        <i>
         The New Yorker
        </i>
        . And, in the film adaptation of his novel
        <i>
         Naked Lunch
        </i>
        , his typewriter is a living, insect-like entity (voiced by North American actor Peter Boretski) and actually dictates the book to him.
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
           <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">
            citation needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Writer Zack Helm and director Mark Forster explored the potential mechanics of the "Soft Typewriter" philosophy in the movie
        <i>
         Stranger than Fiction
        </i>
        , in which the very act of typing up her handwritten notes gives a fiction writer the power to kill or otherwise manipulate her main character in real life.
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
           <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">
            citation needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Hemingway" title="Ernest Hemingway">
         Ernest Hemingway
        </a>
        used to write his books standing up in front of a Royal typewriter suitably placed on a tall bookshelf. This typewriter, still on its bookshelf, is kept in
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Finca_Vigia" title="Finca Vigia">
         Finca Vigia
        </a>
        , Hemingway's
        <a href="/wiki/Havana" title="Havana">
         Havana
        </a>
        house (now a museum) where he lived until 1960, the year before his death.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/J._R._R._Tolkien" title="J. R. R. Tolkien">
         J. R. R. Tolkien
        </a>
        was likewise accustomed to typing from awkward positions: "balancing his typewriter on his attic bed, because there was no room on his desk".
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
         <a href="#cite_note-76">
          [75]
         </a>
        </sup>
        In his Foreword to
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/The_Lord_of_the_Rings" title="The Lord of the Rings">
          The Lord of the Rings
         </a>
        </i>
        , Tolkien stated that "the whole story ... had to be typed, and re-typed: by me; the cost of professional typing by the ten-fingered was beyond my means."
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Jack_Kerouac" title="Jack Kerouac">
         Jack Kerouac
        </a>
        , a fast typist at 100 words per minute, typed
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/On_the_Road" title="On the Road">
          On the Road
         </a>
        </i>
        on a roll of paper so he would not be interrupted by having to change the paper. Within two weeks of starting to write
        <i>
         On the Road
        </i>
        , Kerouac had one single-spaced paragraph, 120 feet long. Some scholars say the scroll was shelf paper; others contend it was a Thermo-fax roll; another theory is that the roll consisted of sheets of architect’s paper taped together.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ja_34-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-ja-34">
          [33]
         </a>
        </sup>
        His rapid work earned the famous rebuke from
        <a href="/wiki/Truman_Capote" title="Truman Capote">
         Truman Capote
        </a>
        , "That's not writing, it's typing."
       </li>
       <li>
        Another fast typist of the
        <a href="/wiki/Beat_Generation" title="Beat Generation">
         Beat Generation
        </a>
        was
        <a href="/wiki/Richard_Brautigan" title="Richard Brautigan">
         Richard Brautigan
        </a>
        , who said that he thought out the plots of his books in detail beforehand, then typed them out at speeds approaching 90 to 100 words a minute.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
         <a href="#cite_note-77">
          [76]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Tom_Robbins" title="Tom Robbins">
         Tom Robbins
        </a>
        waxed philosophical about the Remington SL3, a typewriter that he bought to write
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Still_Life_with_Woodpecker" title="Still Life with Woodpecker">
          Still Life with Woodpecker
         </a>
        </i>
        . He eventually did away with it because it is too complicated and inhuman for the writing of
        <a href="/wiki/Poetry" title="Poetry">
         poetry
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        After completing the novel
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Beautiful_Losers" title="Beautiful Losers">
          Beautiful Losers
         </a>
        </i>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Cohen" title="Leonard Cohen">
         Leonard Cohen
        </a>
        is said to have flung his typewriter into the
        <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">
         Aegean Sea
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
           <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2011)">
            citation needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Don_Marquis" title="Don Marquis">
         Don Marquis
        </a>
        purposely used the limitations of a typewriter (or more precisely, a particular typist) in his
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archy_and_mehitabel" title="Archy and mehitabel">
          archy and mehitabel
         </a>
        </i>
        series of newspaper columns, which were later compiled into a series of books. According to his literary conceit, a
        <a href="/wiki/Cockroach" title="Cockroach">
         cockroach
        </a>
        named "Archy" was a
        <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">
         reincarnated
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Free_verse" title="Free verse">
         free-verse
        </a>
        poet, who would type articles overnight by jumping onto the keys of a manual typewriter. The writings were typed completely in lower case, because of the cockroach's inability to generate the heavy force needed to operate the shift key. The lone exception is the poem "CAPITALS AT LAST" from
        <i>
         archys life of mehitabel
        </i>
        , written in 1933.
       </li>
       <li>
        Author
        <a href="/wiki/Ray_Bradbury" title="Ray Bradbury">
         Ray Bradbury
        </a>
        used a typewriter for rent at the library to write his work known as "
        <a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit_451" title="Fahrenheit 451">
         Fahrenheit 451
        </a>
        ", which was published in 1953.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
         <a href="#cite_note-78">
          [77]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Late_users">
        Late users
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Late users">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Andy_Rooney" title="Andy Rooney">
         Andy Rooney
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/William_F._Buckley_Jr." title="William F. Buckley Jr.">
         William F. Buckley Jr.
        </a>
        (1982) were among many writers who were very reluctant to switch from typewriters to computers.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/David_McCullough" title="David McCullough">
         David McCullough
        </a>
        bought a second-hand Royal typewriter in 1965 and has used it to compose every book he has published.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Hunter_S._Thompson" title="Hunter S. Thompson">
         Hunter S. Thompson
        </a>
        kept a typewriter in his kitchen and is believed to have written his "Hey, Rube!" column for ESPN.com on a typewriter. He used a typewriter until his suicide in 2005.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theodore_Kaczynski" title="Theodore Kaczynski">
         Theodore Kaczynski
        </a>
        , the Unabomber, wrote his
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unabomber_manifesto" title="Unabomber manifesto">
         manifesto
        </a>
        as well as his letters on a manual typewriter.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/David_Sedaris" title="David Sedaris">
         David Sedaris
        </a>
        used a typewriter to write his essay collections through
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Me_Talk_Pretty_One_Day" title="Me Talk Pretty One Day">
          Me Talk Pretty One Day
         </a>
        </i>
        at least.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Richard_Polt" title="Richard Polt">
         Richard Polt
        </a>
        , a philosophy professor at
        <a href="/wiki/Xavier_University" title="Xavier University">
         Xavier Universit
        </a>
        y in Cincinnati who collects typewriters, edits ETCetera, a quarterly magazine about historic writing machines, and is the author of the book
        <a class="external text" href="http://typewriterrevolution.com" rel="nofollow">
         The Typewriter Revolution: A Typist's Companion for the 21st Century
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/William_Gibson" title="William Gibson">
         William Gibson
        </a>
        used a Hermes 2000 model manual typewriter to write
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Neuromancer" title="Neuromancer">
          Neuromancer
         </a>
        </i>
        and half of
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Count_Zero" title="Count Zero">
          Count Zero
         </a>
        </i>
        before a mechanical failure and lack of replacement parts forced him to upgrade to an
        <a href="/wiki/Apple_IIc" title="Apple IIc">
         Apple IIc
        </a>
        computer.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
         <a href="#cite_note-79">
          [78]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Harlan_Ellison" title="Harlan Ellison">
         Harlan Ellison
        </a>
        has used typewriters for his entire career, and when he was no longer able to have them repaired, learned to do it himself; he has repeatedly stated his belief that computers are bad for writing, maintaining, "Art is not supposed to be easier!"
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
         <a href="#cite_note-80">
          [79]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Author
        <a href="/wiki/Cormac_McCarthy" title="Cormac McCarthy">
         Cormac McCarthy
        </a>
        continues to write his novels on an
        <a href="/wiki/Olivetti_Lettera_32" title="Olivetti Lettera 32">
         Olivetti Lettera 32
        </a>
        typewriter to the present day. In 2009, the Lettera he obtained from a pawn shop in 1963, on which nearly all his novels and screenplays have been written, was auctioned for charity at
        <a href="/wiki/Christie%27s" title="Christie's">
         Christie's
        </a>
        for $254,500
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USD" title="USD">
         USD
        </a>
        ;
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
         <a href="#cite_note-81">
          [80]
         </a>
        </sup>
        McCarthy obtained an identical replacement for $20 to continue writing on.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
         <a href="#cite_note-82">
          [81]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Will_Self" title="Will Self">
         Will Self
        </a>
        explains why he uses a manual typewriter: "I think the computer user does their thinking on the screen, and the non-computer user is compelled, because he or she has to retype a whole text, to do a lot more thinking in the head."
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
         <a href="#cite_note-83">
          [82]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Typewriters_in_popular_culture">
        Typewriters in popular culture
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Typewriters in popular culture">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="In_music">
        In music
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: In music">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The composer
        <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Soroz%C3%A1bal" title="Pablo Sorozábal">
         Pablo Sorozábal
        </a>
        includes in a scene of his
        <a href="/wiki/Zarzuela" title="Zarzuela">
         zarzuela
        </a>
        <i>
         La eterna canción
        </i>
        (1945) a typewriter, accompanied by an orchestra and vocal soloists: the scene is in a police station, where a policeman is deposing witnesses, and is singing while he types the report.
       </li>
       <li>
        The composer
        <a href="/wiki/Leroy_Anderson" title="Leroy Anderson">
         Leroy Anderson
        </a>
        wrote
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/The_Typewriter" title="The Typewriter">
          The Typewriter
         </a>
        </i>
        (1950) for orchestra and typewriter, and it has since been used as the theme for numerous radio programs. The solo instrument is a real typewriter played by a
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Percussionist" title="Percussionist">
         percussionist
        </a>
        . The piece was later made famous by comedian
        <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Lewis" title="Jerry Lewis">
         Jerry Lewis
        </a>
        as part of his regular routine both on screen and stage, most notably in the 1963 film
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Who%27s_Minding_the_Store%3F" title="Who's Minding the Store?">
          Who's Minding the Store?
         </a>
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The clacking of typewriter keys can be heard at the beginning of
        <a href="/wiki/Dolly_Parton" title="Dolly Parton">
         Dolly Parton
        </a>
        's song
        <a href="/wiki/9_to_5_(Dolly_Parton_song)" title="9 to 5 (Dolly Parton song)">
         9 to 5
        </a>
        . Parton has said in interviews that when writing the song, to mimic the typing keys sound, she would run her acrylic fingernails back and forth against each other.
       </li>
       <li>
        The song "Embassy Lament" from the 1986 Musical
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Chess_(musical)" title="Chess (musical)">
          Chess
         </a>
        </i>
        mimics the sound of typing in the bridge.
       </li>
       <li>
        A typewriter provides the percussive backing for
        <a href="/wiki/Stereo_Total" title="Stereo Total">
         Stereo Total
        </a>
        's "Dactylo Rock" – the first song from their debut album (1995)
       </li>
       <li>
        A suite of songs entitled "Green Typewriters" is on The Olivia Tremor Control's album
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dusk_At_Cubist_Castle" title="Dusk At Cubist Castle">
          Dusk At Cubist Castle
         </a>
        </i>
        (1996), and the sounds of typewriters can be heard in a few of the sections.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Guster" title="Guster">
         Guster
        </a>
        's 1999 song
        <a href="/wiki/Barrel_of_a_Gun_(Guster_song)" title="Barrel of a Gun (Guster song)">
         Barrel of a Gun
        </a>
        features a typewriter as percussion.
       </li>
       <li>
        American singer-songwriter
        <a href="/wiki/Marian_Call" title="Marian Call">
         Marian Call
        </a>
        accompanies herself on a typewriter on "Nerd Anthem" (c. 1998)
       </li>
       <li>
        American musician
        <a href="/wiki/Beck" title="Beck">
         Beck
        </a>
        's 2005 music video for "Black Tambourine" features typewriter characters to animate Beck's moving and playing guitar.
       </li>
       <li>
        The title track of
        <a href="/wiki/Heernt" title="Heernt">
         Heernt
        </a>
        's 2006 album
        <i>
         Locked in a Basement
        </i>
        prominently features the typewriter as a percussion instrument.
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_Typewriter_Orchestra&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Boston Typewriter Orchestra (page does not exist)">
         Boston Typewriter Orchestra
        </a>
        (BTO) has performed at numerous art festivals, clubs, and parties since 2004.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPR-BTO_84-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-NPR-BTO-84">
          [83]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BTO_85-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-BTO-85">
          [84]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The group consists of a half-dozen performers who use typewriters as
        <a href="/wiki/Percussion_instrument" title="Percussion instrument">
         percussive musical instruments
        </a>
        , under the slogan, "The revolution will be typewritten".
       </li>
       <li>
        South Korean improviser Ryu Hankil frequently performs typewriters, most prominently in his 2009 album"Becoming Typewriter".
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
         <a href="#cite_note-86">
          [85]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Lead singer and songwriter Eddie Vedder of Pearl Jam types many of the band's lyrics on vintage typewriters.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other">
        Other
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Other">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <ul>
       <li>
        In the film
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_History_of_the_Typewriter_recited_by_Michael_Winslow" title="The History of the Typewriter recited by Michael Winslow">
          The History of the Typewriter recited by Michael Winslow
         </a>
        </i>
        , voice sound effect performer
        <a href="/wiki/Michael_Winslow" title="Michael Winslow">
         Michael Winslow
        </a>
        recreates the sounds of 32 typewriters from history.
       </li>
       <li>
        The word "typewriter" is often cited as the longest English word that can be typed using only one row of keys of a QWERTY keyboard. This is untrue, since "
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rupturewort" title="Rupturewort">
         rupturewort
        </a>
        " (a kind of flowering plant) has 11 letters, while "typewriter" has only 10.
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Taber%27s_Cyclopedic_Medical_Dictionary" title="Taber's Cyclopedic Medical Dictionary">
          Taber's Cyclopedic Medical Dictionary
         </a>
        </i>
        defines "
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Uropyoureter&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Uropyoureter (page does not exist)">
         uropyoureter
        </a>
        " (12 letters).
       </li>
       <li>
        A sentence which uses every letter of the alphabet (a
        <a href="/wiki/Pangram" title="Pangram">
         pangram
        </a>
        ), "
        <a href="/wiki/The_quick_brown_fox_jumps_over_the_lazy_dog" title="The quick brown fox jumps over the lazy dog">
         The quick brown fox jumps over the lazy dog
        </a>
        " can be used to check typewriters quickly.
       </li>
       <li>
        The early
        <a href="/wiki/Resident_Evil" title="Resident Evil">
         Resident Evil
        </a>
        video games used a typewriter as the save feature, and used one ink ribbon per save.
       </li>
       <li>
        The opening
        <a href="/wiki/Title_sequence" title="Title sequence">
         title sequence
        </a>
        of
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Murder_She_Wrote" title="Murder She Wrote">
          Murder She Wrote
         </a>
        </i>
        prominently features
        <a href="/wiki/Jessica_Fletcher" title="Jessica Fletcher">
         Jessica Fletcher
        </a>
        touch-typing a manuscript with a 1940s
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_KMM_Manual_Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Royal KMM Manual Typewriter (page does not exist)">
         Royal KMM Manual Typewriter
        </a>
        . Although in one episode Fletcher rejects a character's offer to sell her a computer to replace the old Royal (which he calls a "dinosaur"), towards the series end, she, too begins using a computer and word-processing typewriter.
       </li>
       <li>
        In
        <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">
         Rome
        </a>
        the
        <a href="/wiki/Altare_della_Patria" title="Altare della Patria">
         Altare della Patria
        </a>
        , National Monument to King
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victor_Emmanuel_II" title="Victor Emmanuel II">
         Victor Emmanuel II
        </a>
        , used to be nicknamed "the typewriter" (
        <i>
         la macchina per scrivere
        </i>
        in Italian) because of its strange shape and popular dislike toward it.
       </li>
       <li>
        The 2012 French comedy movie
        <i>
         Populaire
        </i>
        , starring Romain Duris and Déborah François, centers around a young secretary in the 1950s striving to win typewriting speed competitions.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
         <a href="#cite_note-87">
          [86]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        2012 AU Education Research claimed that proper typing position and distance to the screen are the main factors of typing faster.
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
           <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">
            citation needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Forensic_examination">
        Forensic examination
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
