        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Forensic examination">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Typewritten documents may be examined by
       <a href="/wiki/Questioned_document_examination" title="Questioned document examination">
        forensic document examiners
       </a>
       . This is done primarily to determine 1) the make and/or model of the typewriter used to produce a document, or 2) whether or not a particular suspect typewriter might have been used to produce a document.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelly_88-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kelly-88">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In some situations, an ink or correction ribbon may also be examined.
      </p>
      <p>
       The determination of a make and/or model of typewriter is a 'classification' problem and several systems have been developed for this purpose.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelly_88-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kelly-88">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These include the original Haas Typewriter Atlases (Pica version)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
        <a href="#cite_note-89">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and (Non-Pica version)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the TYPE system developed by Dr. Philip Bouffard,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
        <a href="#cite_note-91">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Canadian_Mounted_Police" title="Royal Canadian Mounted Police">
        Royal Canadian Mounted Police
       </a>
       's Termatrex Typewriter classification system,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
        <a href="#cite_note-92">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Interpol" title="Interpol">
        Interpol
       </a>
       's Typewriter classification system,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
        <a href="#cite_note-93">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       among others.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelly_88-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kelly-88">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Because of the tolerances of the mechanical parts, slight variation in the alignment of the letters and their uneven wear, each typewriter has an individual "signature" or "
       <a href="/wiki/Fingerprint" title="Fingerprint">
        fingerprint
       </a>
       ", which may permit a typewritten document to be
       <a href="/wiki/Forensic_identification" title="Forensic identification">
        traced back
       </a>
       to the typewriter on which it was produced. For devices utilizing replaceable components, such as a typeball element, any association may be restricted to a specific element, rather than to the typewriter as a whole.
      </p>
      <p>
       The earliest reference in fictional literature to the potential identification of a typewriter as having produced a document was by Sir Arthur Conan Doyle who wrote "A Case of Identity" in 1891.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crown_94-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-crown-94">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In non-fiction, the first
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forensic_document_examination" title="Forensic document examination">
        document examiner
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crown_94-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-crown-94">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       to describe how a typewriter might be identified was William E. Hagan who wrote, in 1894, "All typewriter machines, even when using the same kind of type, become more or less peculiar by use as to the work done by them".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
        <a href="#cite_note-95">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Other early discussions of the topic were provided by
       <a href="/wiki/Albert_S._Osborn" title="Albert S. Osborn">
        A. S. Osborn
       </a>
       in his 1908 treatise,
       <i>
        Typewriting as Evidence
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
        <a href="#cite_note-96">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and again in his 1929 textbook,
       <i>
        Questioned Documents
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
        <a href="#cite_note-97">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A modern description of the examination procedure is laid out in ASTM Standard E2494-08 (Standard Guide for Examination of Typewritten Items).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astm_TW_98-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-astm_TW-98">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Typewriter examination was used in the
       <a href="/wiki/Leopold_and_Loeb" title="Leopold and Loeb">
        Leopold and Loeb
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Alger_Hiss" title="Alger Hiss">
        Alger Hiss
       </a>
       cases. In the
       <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">
        Eastern Bloc
       </a>
       , typewriters (together with
       <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">
        printing presses
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copy_machine" title="Copy machine">
        copy machines
       </a>
       , and later
       <a href="/wiki/Printer_(computing)" title="Printer (computing)">
        computer printers
       </a>
       ) were a controlled technology, with
       <a href="/wiki/Secret_police" title="Secret police">
        secret police
       </a>
       in charge of maintaining files of the typewriters and their owners. In the
       <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
        Soviet Union
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/First_Department" title="First Department">
        First Department
       </a>
       of each organization sent data on organization's typewriters to the
       <a href="/wiki/KGB" title="KGB">
        KGB
       </a>
       . This posed a significant risk for
       <a href="/wiki/Dissident" title="Dissident">
        dissidents
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Samizdat" title="Samizdat">
        samizdat
       </a>
       authors. In
       <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Republic_of_Romania" title="Socialist Republic of Romania">
        Romania
       </a>
       , according to State Council Decree No. 98 of March 28, 1983, owning a typewriter, both by businesses or by private persons, was subject to an approval given by the local police authorities.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Betea_99-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Betea-99">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
       People previously convicted of any crime or those who because of their behaviour were considered to be "a danger to public order or to the security of the state" were refused approval.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Betea_99-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Betea-99">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In addition, once a year, typewriter owners had to take the typewriter to the local police station, where they would be asked to type down a sample of all the typewriter's characters.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Betea_99-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Betea-99">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was also forbidden to borrow, lend, or repair typewriters other than at the places that had been authorized by the police.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Betea_99-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Betea-99">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The ribbon can be read vertically, although only if it has not been typed over more than once. This can be very hard to do as it does not include spaces, but can be done, giving every typewriter a "memory".
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">
        See also
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: See also">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>

        <li id="cite_note-60">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-60">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation journal">
           Liebowitz, S. J.; Stephen E. Margolis (1990).
           <a class="external text" href="http://wwwpub.utdallas.edu/~liebowit/keys1.html" rel="nofollow">
            "The Fable of the Keys"
           </a>
           .
           <i>
            Journal of Law &amp; Economics
           </i>
           . The University of Chicago. XXXIII (April 1990).
           <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">
            doi
           </a>
           :
           <a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1086%2F467198" rel="nofollow">
            10.1086/467198
           </a>
           <span class="reference-accessdate">
            . Retrieved
            <span class="nowrap">
             2008-06-18
            </span>
           </span>
           .
           <q>
            This article examines the history, economics, and ergonomics of the typewriter keyboard. We show that David's version of the history of the market's rejection of Dvorak does not report the true history, and we present evidence that the continued use of Qwerty is efficient given the current understanding of keyboard design.
           </q>
          </cite>

          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Type+Rules%3A+The+Designer%27s+Guide+to+Professional+Typography&amp;rft.place=New+Jersey&amp;rft.pages=199&amp;rft.edition=3rd&amp;rft.pub=John+Wiley+%26+Sons&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-470-54251-4&amp;rft.aulast=Strizver&amp;rft.aufirst=Ilene&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATypewriter">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
          . Strizver states that "When available, true primes should be used for measurements, but typewriter quotes (not smart quotes) have become the accepted practice in digital typography."
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-70">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-70">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation web">
           Regents of the University of Minnesota (18 July 2007).
           <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20090117122230/http://www1.umn.edu/urelate/style/copyprep.html" rel="nofollow">
            "University of Minnesota Style Manual"
           </a>
           .
           <i>
            University of Minnesota
           </i>
           . Regents of the University of Minnesota. Archived from
           <a class="external text" href="http://www1.umn.edu/urelate/style/copyprep.html#Anchor-Copy-47857" rel="nofollow">
            the original
           </a>
           on January 17, 2009
           <span class="reference-accessdate">
            . Retrieved
            <span class="nowrap">
             12 May
            </span>
            2010
           </span>
           .
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=University+of+Minnesota&amp;rft.atitle=University+of+Minnesota+Style+Manual&amp;rft.date=2007-07-18&amp;rft.au=Regents+of+the+University+of+Minnesota&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww1.umn.edu%2Furelate%2Fstyle%2Fcopyprep.html%23Anchor-Copy-47857&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATypewriter">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
          This topic is discussed under "Creating Professional-looking Text."; Williams 2003. pps. 31, 33. Another example of the limitation of the typewriter in regard to underlining, was the necessity to underline the titles of books and stand-alone works in Bibliographies—works that would otherwise have been italicized, if that capability existed on the typewriter.
         </span>
        </li>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Patents">
        Patents
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Typewriter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Patents">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <span>
         <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/patents/US79265" rel="nofollow">
          US79265
         </a>
        </span>
        – Improvement in Type-Writing Machines (the patent that laid the basis for the Sholes &amp; Glidden Type Writer)
       </li>
       <li>
        <span>
         <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/patents/US349026" rel="nofollow">
          US349026
         </a>
        </span>
        – typewriter ribbon, by George K. Anderson of Memphis, Tennessee.
       </li>
